     ‚Ä¶ +83 lines (ctrl+o to expand)
‚óŹ Now, let me bring perspective on the four areas.

  Reading through what Vaibhav described and mapping it against what you
  described on the call about your internal practice, I see something important
  about how these four consumer groups relate to each other architecturally.
  Vaibhav framed them as four separate consumers, but when you look at what each
   one actually needs, the picture is more like a single visual comparison
  engine with four different input/output configurations layered on top:

  - QE is the broadest consumer. They want the full tool: exploratory testing,
  visual regression, localization, the works. This is the core platform.
  - Development teams want the same visual comparison engine, but their baseline
   is a Figma design file instead of a prior screenshot. The engine compares
  rendered UI against a design spec rather than against a previous state.
  - UI/UX wants the same engine again, but with configurable thresholds,
  component-level baselines, and an approval routing workflow on top. They are
  the governance layer over visual standards.
  - Producers want the simplest version: did my content publish correctly?
  Compare what is live against what should be live. The engine is the same, the
  inputs are different (CMS-published content vs. expected content).

  The unified backend you proposed on the call maps perfectly to this. The
  differentiation across the four groups is really about three things: what the
  baseline reference is (prior screenshot, Figma design, component standard,
  expected content state), what the tolerance level is (pixel-exact for UI/UX,
  looser for exploratory QE, content-focused for producers), and what happens
  with the output (QE gets bug reports, developers get self-check feedback,
  UI/UX gets approval routing, producers get validation reports).

  Here's my question: When you think about framing this in the document for
  Vaibhav, do you want to lead with the unified platform concept and then show
  how each consumer group plugs into it, or do you want to lead with the four
  consumer groups as Vaibhav described them and then reveal the unified
  architecture as BayOne's insight? The first approach is more technically
  direct. The second mirrors how Vaibhav thinks about it and positions BayOne as
   having listened first and then added value with the architectural
  recommendation.

‚úĽ Worked for 1m 25s

‚ĚĮ I think we should do the second approach: lead with the four consumer groups  
  as ViPoP described, and then reveal a unified architecture as BayOne's        
  insight. Whenever we talk about the unified architecture, make it clear that  
  these are modular, reusable components. They can be built out progressively   
  so that you don't need to, or Sephora doesn't need to do a giant project or   
  try to boil the ocean. It's just the idea that there is a unified backbone    
  that is shared across all, that can be built up and is generically useful     
  across all of them.                                                           
                                                                                
  For instance, don't make it sound like QE's needs are completely separate     
  from the development teams and producers. It will just be an ecosystem, and   
  everyone can have their own access to tools to that. For instance, like the   
  teams that want to use Figma, completely fine; we can combine MCP with        
  Playwright for Figma and build custom connectors for that.                    
                                                                                
  We want to be detailed as well and think about everything that was said       
  across all of the transcripts by Colin specifically. A lot of great design    
  ideas there, and you can see how ViPoP responded to that.

      10 ## Document Structure Decision
     ‚Ä¶ +63 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Now, let me work through the four consumer groups with you. I've read through
  everything you said across both transcripts, and I want to start with the one
  I think is the most interesting architecturally.

  Consumer Group 3: UI/UX Team is the most technically specified of the four.
  Vaibhav gave three distinct capabilities: pixel-level differential thresholds,
   baseline components, and approval routing. What makes this group interesting
  is that the features Vaibhav described for UI/UX are actually the governance
  and configuration layer that sits on top of the core visual comparison engine
  that all four groups share.

  Think about it this way: every consumer group needs visual comparison. But the
   UI/UX team is the one that decides how sensitive that comparison is
  (thresholds), what the reference standard is (baselines), and what happens 
  when something deviates (approval routing). In a real deployment, the UI/UX
  team's configuration would likely inform how the other three groups use the
  tool. If the UI/UX team defines component baselines and threshold tolerances,
  those same baselines and tolerances can be inherited by QE for regression
  testing, by development teams for self-checks, and by producers for content
  validation.

  This maps directly to something you said in the March 24 meeting about
  standards-setting: "We can help to set those standards first early on, at
  least the first pass that you get to guide the unit test and the way that
  they're created." The UI/UX baseline and threshold configuration is
  essentially standards-setting for visual quality, applied the same way you
  described standards-setting for test generation.

  The review agent concept you described in Set 02 ("smart but lazy, only shown
  back to me after the agents are done, if and only if it looks good") maps
  naturally to the approval routing Vaibhav wants. The review agent is the first
   stage of routing: it filters before a human even sees the discrepancy. Then
  the approval workflow routes what passes the review agent to the right human
  decision-maker.

  My question: In the document, should we position the UI/UX team's threshold
  and baseline configuration as the governance layer that cascades down to the
  other three groups, or should we keep each group's description self-contained
  and let the unified architecture section at the end make the connections? The
  first option reveals the ecosystem thinking earlier but might get ahead of
  where Vaibhav is in his own mental model. The second keeps the listening-first
   structure clean and saves the architectural insight for the payoff at the
  end.

      10 ## Sequencing Approach for the Document
     ‚Ä¶ +95 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Now let me go deep on the QE content. I'm going to re-read both transcripts

  Read 2 files (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Good. After going through both transcripts deeply, I have a much clearer
  picture of the QE consumer group. Here's my understanding.

  Sephora's QE organization was the "slowest horse in the race" when Vaibhav
  took it over. His mandate is velocity parity: testing cannot be slower than
  development, especially now that the AMP model means small, frequent code
  churn. They're a Selenium shop migrating to Playwright (POCs done, new work
  already on Playwright), they have an AI mandate since August 2025, and their
  QE COE under Deepika has been building agents across the QE lifecycle:
  validating acceptance criteria, generating test cases, automating tests, CI/CD
   continuous testing. Visual QA is one of several gaps they've identified but
  haven't been able to close yet.

  For the QE section of the document, the point is not to explain what QE is.
  Vaibhav knows QE. The point is to show that BayOne understands the real
  day-to-day of a QE team going through this exact transformation, and that our
  approach addresses it:

  - The Selenium-to-Playwright transition is not just swapping frameworks.
  Playwright is faster, more reliable, eliminates the delay and uncertainty
  problems Selenium has, works natively with AI, and is Microsoft-native which
  fits Sephora's Azure environment. BayOne already builds on Playwright
  internally.
  - Exploratory testing becomes agentic. Instead of a human spending hours
  clicking through an application looking for visual bugs, an agent does the
  exploration, logs every step, captures screenshots, notes anomalies, and only
  surfaces results worth human attention through the review layer.
  - Regression testing becomes deterministic playbooks. AI discovers effective
  test flows, they get saved as repeatable, replayable playbooks tied to code
  changes and releases. When something ships, the right tests fire
  automatically. Tests do not break unless the underlying feature actually
  changed.
  - CI/CD integration means testing is not a separate manual step. It is woven
  into the development and release pipeline through GitHub Actions, with
  orchestration at scale through Airflow for nightly regression suites or
  post-deploy validation.
  - Human-in-the-loop with confidence scoring directly addresses the trust
  problem Vaibhav described (40-50% confidence now, wanting to get to 80%+). The
   reinforcement approach builds trust incrementally through validated
  successes, and human involvement decreases over time, not all at once.
  - Traceability and observability mean the QE leads who are transforming from
  engineers to decision-makers actually have the data to manage agent teams, not
   just faith that things are working.

  All of this runs in Docker containers for isolation and reproducibility,
  connects to repositories through the control panel, and is built on a stack
  the team can understand and maintain.

  I think I have enough from this discussion to draft the document. We've
  covered the four-section structure, the ecosystem framing, the three
  engagement models, the full stack, the playbook concept, the control panel,
  the QE approach, the sequencing recommendation, the depth calibration, and the
   tone. Before I move to drafting, is there anything else you want to talk
  through, or should I propose an outline for the document and then start
  writing?

  deterministically, tie to releases). The standard stack in a table:
  Playwright, LangGraph, Azure AI Foundry, Airflow, GitHub Actions,
  Django/FastAPI. Human-in-the-loop as a design feature with confidence building
   over time.

  BayOne uses this internally ("practice like we play"). Stack alignment
  (Vaibhav endorsed all three core technologies). "Boring is good" philosophy,
  Microsoft-native, no exotic platforms. Python-native backend for seamless
  agentic library integration. Three engagement models in cards (fully managed,
  variable split, pure staffing), with the outcome-based vs. headcount
  distinction.

  Opens with a disclaimer that this is a high-level take without a deep dive.
  Production architecture: Azure Container Apps, Azure Postgres, Azure AI
  Foundry, Azure Redis, pay-by-usage scaling. Deterministic-first philosophy.

  Control panel (user-scoped, repo integration, SSO). Docker for development
  isolation.

  Producers as low-hanging fruit. QE or UI/UX for highest cross-board value.

  sequencing recommendation requires a deeper dive. Offer to help with Open
  WebUI if desired.

  Section 06: Next Steps
  Requirements deep dive with Vaibhav and Deepika. What BayOne needs to sharpen
  the recommendation. Closing statement as a partner.

‚óŹ Architecture content document is at
  planning/architecture_content_draft_2026-04-14.md.

  This is structured as five layers that build on each other:

  1. Deterministic Automation ‚ÄĒ Playwright, codebase mapping, CI/CD integration,
   Docker isolation
  2. Saved Playbooks ‚ÄĒ AI-discovered workflows converted to deterministic,
  replayable, release-mapped assets
  3. Agentic Exploration ‚ÄĒ AI for new features, unfamiliar states, visual
  understanding, with the discovery-to-playbook cycle
  4. Review and Confidence ‚ÄĒ Review agent filtering, reinforcement-based
  confidence scoring, human-validated track records
  5. Observability ‚ÄĒ Full traceability, aggregate performance data, edge case
  diagnostics for QE leadership

  Then it covers how configuration (baseline reference, tolerance, workflow
  integration, tool connectors) lets one architecture serve all four consumer
  groups.

  The playbook engine and human-in-the-loop sections follow with more detail,
  drawing from the specific design ideas across both transcripts and the
  discussion.

  Take a look and let me know what you want to adjust. We will work through this
   section-by-section.

          -orkflow integration.</p>

          +eeper discussion with the team.</p>

          + Pod (AMP) model: lean two-to-three person teams delivering sm
          +all, frequent enhancements in a continuous development cycle.

          +er testing. Quality engineering must operate at the same pace

          +han catching up after the fact. The organization has already d

          +es by March. Visual quality engineering is the next capability
          + needed to sustain that pace.</p>

          +d workflow integration.</p>

      573        <h2>Proposed Architecture</h2>
      513 -      <p class="lead">BayOne recommends a unified platform arc
          -hitecture: a single system with modular, reusable components t
          -hat serves all four consumer groups through configurable setti
          -ngs rather than separate tools or parallel implementations.</p
          ->                                                             
      574 +      <p class="lead">BayOne recommends an architecture that i
          +s deterministic at its foundation, agentic where judgment is r
          +equired, and configurable across the teams that use it. The pl
          +atform is a single ecosystem designed for long-term ownership 
          +and personalization, built from modular components that the or
          +ganization can understand, maintain, and extend independently.
          +</p>                                                          
      575  
      515 -      <h3>Unified Backbone with Configurable Specificity</h3> 
      516 -      <p>The platform provides one visual comparison and testi
          -ng engine. What varies across consumer groups is not the under
          -lying technology but three configuration dimensions:</p>      
      576 +      <h3>Layered Architecture</h3>                           
      577 +      <p>The platform is organized in layers, each handling th
          +e part of the problem it is best suited for. The lower layers 
          +are deterministic and predictable. The upper layers introduce 
          +intelligence. This separation means the foundation operates re
          +liably independent of AI performance, and the AI layer adds ca
          +pability without introducing variability into the critical pat
          +h. When performance plateaus, the constraint is typically in t
          +he framework and the deterministic foundation, not in the AI m
          +odels. Investing in framework quality yields more than investi
          +ng in better models.</p>

      682 +                                                              
      683 +      <h4>Layer 1: Deterministic Automation</h4>              
      684 +      <p>Playwright drives all browser interaction. When the p
          +latform clicks a button, navigates a page, captures a screensh
          +ot, or compares two visual states, it does so deterministicall
          +y. There is no AI interpretation in the action itself. Playwri
          +ght handles desktop and mobile viewports, integrates natively 
          +with cross-browser testing infrastructure, and is Microsoft-na
          +tive, aligning with the existing Azure environment.</p>       
      685 +      <p>At this layer, the platform also maintains a determin
          +istic map of the application under test: how different parts o
          +f the codebase relate to each other, which UI components depen
          +d on which code paths, and which visual tests are relevant to 
          +which changes. This mapping extends beyond surface-level file 
          +associations to recursive downstream dependencies, so that a c
          +hange in one module triggers tests for everything that module 
          +affects, not just the module itself. This feeds directly into 
          +CI/CD integration: when code changes ship, the platform automa
          +tically identifies which tests to run rather than executing th
          +e entire suite or relying on manual test selection.</p>       
      686 +      <p>All test execution occurs in isolated environments. D
          +ocker containers provide clean, reproducible execution states 
          +for development and testing. In production, this translates to
          + Azure Container Apps with compute allocated based on actual d
          +emand.</p>                                                    
      687 +                                                              
      688 +      <h4>Layer 2: Saved Playbooks and Structured Testing</h4>
      689 +      <p>Once an effective testing workflow has been discovere
          +d and validated, it is saved as a deterministic playbook: an e
          +xact, repeatable sequence of steps, assertions, and validation
          + criteria. Playbooks do not require AI to execute. They are th
          +e platform's equivalent of recorded automation workflows, exce
          +pt they are tied to the codebase and mapped to specific featur
          +es, UI components, and code paths.</p>                        
      690 +      <p>When a release ships or a code change merges, the pla
          +tform knows which playbooks are affected and runs them automat
          +ically. If a feature changes intentionally, the associated pla
          +ybooks can be updated or retired by the team. Tests do not bre
          +ak on deployment unless the underlying behavior genuinely chan
          +ged.</p>                                                      
      691 +      <p>Scheduled execution through a workflow orchestration 
          +layer (Apache Airflow) handles nightly regression suites, post
          +-deploy validation sweeps, and periodic production monitoring.
          + CI/CD integration through GitHub Actions handles event-driven
          + test triggers on code changes, giving development teams a sea
          +mless experience within their existing workflow.</p>          
      692 +      <p>The accumulated playbook library grows over time and 
          +represents a compounding asset: each validated playbook is a t
          +esting workflow that no longer requires manual execution.</p> 
      693 +                                                              
      694 +      <h4>Layer 3: Agentic Exploration and Discovery</h4>     
      695 +      <p>When the platform encounters a new feature, an unfami
          +liar application state, or a testing objective that no existin
          +g playbook covers, an agentic workflow takes over. The agent u
          +ses its tools (Playwright for browser interaction, visual comp
          +arison models for understanding what it sees, design file refe
          +rences for baseline comparison) to explore, discover anomalies
          +, and construct new testing workflows.</p>                    
      696 +      <p>Exploratory testing is the most visible agentic capab
          +ility. An agent navigates the application as a user would: vis
          +iting pages, interacting with elements, identifying visual inc
          +onsistencies, broken layouts, content errors, or accessibility
          + issues. Every action is logged, every screenshot is captured,
          + and the results are traceable to the specific environment sta
          +te and code version.</p>                                      
      697 +      <p>Visual understanding models distinguish between inten
          +tional design changes and genuine defects. A button that moved
          + slightly due to a layout adjustment is not the same as a butt
          +on that disappeared. This contextual judgment reduces false po
          +sitives that would otherwise require human triage. At scale, a
          +utomated visual consistency exceeds what human reviewers can m
          +aintain, particularly across large volumes of pages, variants,
          + and devices where attention fatigue degrades accuracy.</p>   
      698 +      <p>When an agent discovers a workflow that successfully 
          +validates a testing objective, that workflow becomes a candida
          +te for Layer 2. Once validated by a human reviewer, it is save
          +d as a deterministic playbook. This cycle is what makes the pl
          +atform self-improving: AI explores and discovers, validated di
          +scoveries become deterministic assets, the deterministic libra
          +ry grows, and the need for exploration decreases over time for
          + known areas while remaining available for new features and ch
          +anges.</p>                                                    
      699 +                                                              
      700 +      <div class="highlight-box">                             
      701 +        <p><strong>Guardrails and production safety:</strong> 
          +Agents operating in production are restricted to non-destructi
          +ve actions. They can observe, capture, compare, and report, bu
          +t they cannot modify data, submit forms, or alter application 
          +state. This restriction is enforced at the tool access level, 
          +not through behavioral instructions. In sandbox environments, 
          +destructive actions can be explicitly enabled for testing scen
          +arios that require interaction (form submissions, checkout flo
          +ws, account creation). The platform adapts its permission mode
          +l based on the environment it is operating in.</p>            
      702 +      </div>                                                  
      703 +                                                              
      704 +      <h4>Layer 4: Review and Confidence</h4>                 
      705 +      <p>Before any agent-generated result reaches a human, it
          + passes through a review layer. A dedicated review agent evalu
          +ates the output for quality, relevance, and actionability. Res
          +ults that are noise (false positives, known acceptable variati
          +ons, duplicate findings) are filtered. Only findings that meet
          + a quality threshold are surfaced to human reviewers.</p>     
      706 +      <p>Confidence scoring operates across the entire platfor
          +m. Every agent maintains a track record built from human-valid
          +ated outcomes. When a human approves an agent's finding, that 
          +approval reinforces the pattern and lowers the threshold for f
          +uture human review of similar findings. When a human rejects a
          + finding or corrects an agent, the correction feeds back to ma
          +ke the agent more cautious in that specific area, and the conf
          +idence threshold for that task type increases temporarily unti
          +l the correction is validated through subsequent successful ru
          +ns. This bidirectional feedback (approvals lower thresholds, c
          +orrections raise them) ensures the system calibrates itself to
          + real-world accuracy standards.</p>                           
      707 +      <p>Agents do not assess themselves or each other. Confid
          +ence scores are derived from human-validated outcomes and defi
          +ned performance indicators, not from agent self-evaluation. Th
          +is is a deliberate design choice: agent self-assessment tends 
          +to oscillate between extreme optimism and extreme pessimism ra
          +ther than producing calibrated judgments. Human expertise rema
          +ins structurally necessary for course corrections.</p>        
      708 +                                                              
      709 +      <h4>Layer 5: Observability</h4>                         
      710 +      <p>The platform provides full visibility into what is ha
          +ppening across all layers. Every action is traceable: which ag
          +ent performed it, what tools it used, what environment it oper
          +ated in, what code version was being tested, and what the outc
          +ome was. This traceability is the same standard applied to hum
          +an QE teams and provides the audit trail that enterprise quali
          +ty engineering requires.</p>                                  
      711 +      <p>Beyond individual action tracing, the platform surfac
          +es aggregate data: agent performance trends, test pass rates o
          +ver time, edge case patterns, confidence score trajectories, a
          +nd playbook coverage metrics. When the system is mature, the m
          +ost interesting data is the edge cases. Are remaining failures
          + because the system is too strict? Because the test criteria n
          +eed updating? Because genuine defects are being found? This di
          +agnostic capability helps the team continuously calibrate the 
          +platform against real-world quality standards.</p>            
      712 +      <p>When tests miss something, those edge cases are captu
          +red and fed back into the system as patterns to watch for in t
          +he future, even in situations that are not identical to the or
          +iginal miss. This learning loop expands the platform's awarene
          +ss over time, complementing the confidence scoring mechanism t
          +hat operates at the individual agent level.</p>               
      713 +      <p>This observability layer is designed for the organiza
          +tional transition already underway: QE leadership moving from 
          +hands-on engineering to decision-making and oversight. The pla
          +tform provides the evidence base needed to manage automated te
          +sting operations with confidence, answering the question of wh
          +ether the testing system is healthy, accurate, and improving r
          +ather than whether individual tests passed.</p>               
      714 +                                                              
      715 +      <h3>Configuration Across Consumer Groups</h3>           
      716 +      <p>The layered architecture is the same for all consumer
          + groups. What differs is configuration across four dimensions:
          +</p>                                                          
      717 +                                                              
      718        <div class="workflow-grid">
      719          <div class="workflow-step">
      720            <div class="workflow-step-number">1</div>
     ...
      723          </div>
      724          <div class="workflow-step">
      725            <div class="workflow-step-number">2</div>
      526 -          <h5>Tolerance Level</h5>                            
      527 -          <p>Pixel-level precision for UI/UX governance. Broad
          -er tolerance for QE exploratory testing. Content-level accurac
          -y for publishing validation. Tolerance is a setting, adjustabl
          -e per team, per test type, or per component.</p>              
      726 +          <h5>Tolerance and Precision</h5>                    
      727 +          <p>Pixel-level precision for UI/UX governance. Broad
          +er tolerance for QE exploratory testing. Content-level accurac
          +y for publishing validation. Adjustable per team, per test typ
          +e, or per component.</p>                                      
      728          </div>
      729          <div class="workflow-step">
      730            <div class="workflow-step-number">3</div>
      531 -          <h5>Output and Workflow</h5>                        
      532 -          <p>QE receives test reports and anomaly logs. Develo
          -pment receives self-check feedback. UI/UX receives threshold-b
          -ased alerts with approval routing. Content production receives
          - validation reports. Each output channel is a module on the sa
          -me backbone.</p>                                              
      731 +          <h5>Workflow Integration</h5>                       
      732 +          <p>QE receives test reports integrated with their pi
          +peline. Development receives CI/CD-triggered feedback. UI/UX r
          +eceives threshold alerts with approval routing. Content produc
          +tion receives publishing validation reports.</p>              
      733          </div>
      734        </div>
      735  
      536 -      <h3>Progressive Buildout</h3>

          -ntally. Each consumer group benefits from what was built for t
          -he others, and new capabilities compound rather than duplicate
          -. Sephora can start with one consumer group, validate the appr

          +ence design files as baselines. Teams that work with content m

          +g that every team operates from the same data. The hub integra

          +or while remaining fully traceable and governable. No single a

      560 -      <p>This approach combines the strength of AI (exploratio
          -n, discovery, adaptability) with the reliability of determinis
          -tic automation (repeatability, predictability, auditability). 
          -Tests do not break when features or UI elements change unless 
          -the change genuinely affects the validated behavior. Developme
          -nt teams retain full control over playbook governance.</p>    
      765 +      <p>Playbooks are assets owned by the teams, not by the A
          +I. Teams can review the full sequence of actions in any playbo
          +ok, edit steps, add assertions, adjust tolerances, or retire p
          +laybooks that are no longer relevant. The platform makes testi
          +ng more efficient without removing human governance over what 
          +is tested and how.</p>                                        
      766  
      562 -      <h3>Human-in-the-Loop and Confidence Scoring</h3>       
      563 -      <p>Human involvement is a design feature of the platform
          -, not a limitation. The system follows a reinforcement-based c
          -onfidence model: human reviewers validate agent-generated resu
          -lts in the initial phase, each validated success builds the ag
          -ent's track record, and as the track record grows, the thresho
          -ld for human review decreases. Over time, human involvement sh
          -ifts from reviewing every result to reviewing only exceptions 
          -and edge cases.</p>                                           
      564 -      <p>A review agent layer sits between the testing agents 
          -and human reviewers. This layer filters results before any hum
          -an sees them, ensuring that human attention is spent on genuin
          -e issues rather than noise. The combination of confidence scor
          -ing and review filtering means the system becomes more efficie
          -nt over time without sacrificing oversight.</p>               
      565 -                                                              
      566 -      <h3>Control Panel</h3>                                  
      567 -      <p>The platform includes a web-based control panel scope
          -d to each user's role and responsibilities. The control panel 
          -connects to code repositories, integrates with existing single
          - sign-on infrastructure, and provides visibility into test sta
          -tus, playbook management, and configuration settings.</p>     
      568 -      <p>The recommended backend framework is Python-based (Dj
          -ango or FastAPI), selected for direct compatibility with the a
          -gentic libraries that power the platform. This eliminates tran
          -slation layers between the web application and the agent infra
          -structure. If the team has a strong preference for an alternat
          -ive framework, BayOne can adapt accordingly.</p>              
      767 +      <h3>Human-in-the-Loop as a Design Feature</h3>          
      768 +      <p>Human involvement is a design feature that makes the 
          +system trustworthy, not a limitation to be minimized. In the i
          +nitial phase, human reviewers validate all agent-generated res
          +ults and newly discovered workflows. Each validated success bu
          +ilds the agent's track record. As the record grows, the thresh
          +old for mandatory human review decreases for specific agent an
          +d task combinations that have proven reliable.</p>            
      769 +      <p>The result is a system where human effort decreases n
          +aturally as trust is earned, not because a configuration was c
          +hanged. The human role transitions from reviewing every result
          + to overseeing the system's overall health, intervening on exc
          +eptions, and making course corrections when agents drift. This
          + mirrors the broader organizational transition already underwa
          +y: from hands-on quality engineering to decision-making and ov
          +ersight.</p>

      819        <h3>Deterministic-First Design</h3>
      617 -      <p>The platform follows a deterministic-first design pri
          -nciple. Deterministic processes handle everything they can han
          -dle reliably: Playwright executes browser actions with precisi
          -on, saved playbooks replay exact sequences without interpretat
          -ion, and CI/CD triggers fire based on mapped code changes. AI 
          -is layered on top for work that requires judgment: exploring u
          -nfamiliar application states, interpreting visual discrepancie
          -s, understanding whether a UI change is intentional or anomalo
          -us, and generating new testing workflows.</p>                 
      820 +      <p>The platform follows a deterministic-first design pri
          +nciple. Deterministic processes handle everything they can han
          +dle reliably: Playwright executes browser actions with precisi
          +on, saved playbooks replay exact sequences without interpretat
          +ion, and CI/CD triggers fire based on mapped code changes and 
          +recursive dependency analysis. AI is layered on top for work t
          +hat requires judgment: exploring unfamiliar application states
          +, interpreting visual discrepancies, understanding whether a U
          +I change is intentional or anomalous, and generating new testi
          +ng workflows.</p>                                             
      821        <p>This layering keeps the reliability floor high. The d
           eterministic foundation operates at near-100% consistency. The
            AI layer adds capability without introducing variability into
            the critical path.</p>

       65  ## 02 ‚ÄĒ Proposed Architecture
       66  
       50 -BayOne recommends a unified platform architecture: a single sy
          -stem with modular, reusable components that serves all four co
          -nsumer groups through configurable settings rather than separa
          -te tools or parallel implementations.                         
       67 +BayOne recommends an architecture that is deterministic at its
          + foundation, agentic where judgment is required, and configura
          +ble across the teams that use it. The platform is a single eco
          +system designed for long-term ownership and personalization, b
          +uilt from modular components that the organization can underst
          +and, maintain, and extend independently.                      
       68  
       52 -### Unified Backbone with Configurable Specificity            
       69 +### Layered Architecture                                      
       70  
       54 -The platform provides one visual comparison and testing engine
          -. What varies across consumer groups is not the underlying tec
          -hnology but three configuration dimensions:                   
       71 +The platform is organized in layers, each handling the part of
          + the problem it is best suited for. The lower layers are deter
          +ministic and predictable. The upper layers introduce intellige
          +nce. This separation means the foundation operates reliably in
          +dependent of AI performance, and the AI layer adds capability 
          +without introducing variability into the critical path. When p
          +erformance plateaus, the constraint is typically in the framew
          +ork and the deterministic foundation, not in the AI models. In
          +vesting in framework quality yields more than investing in bet
          +ter models.                                                   
       72  
       56 -**Baseline reference.** The QE team compares against prior app
          -lication state. Development teams compare against Figma design
          - specifications. The UI/UX team compares against defined compo
          -nent standards. The content production team compares published
          - content against expected content state. The same comparison e
          -ngine handles all four; the baseline input is the variable.   
       73 +[Architecture Diagram: Five layers from Deterministic Automati
          +on through Observability, with a Configuration Layer spanning 
          +all]                                                          
       74  
       58 -**Tolerance level.** The UI/UX team may require pixel-level pr
          -ecision with configurable thresholds. The QE team running expl
          -oratory tests may operate with broader tolerance to focus on f
          -unctional anomalies rather than pixel variations. The content 
          -production team needs content-level accuracy (correct images, 
          -correct placement, correct attributes). Tolerance is a setting
          -, adjustable per team, per test type, or per component.       
       75 +#### Layer 1: Deterministic Automation                        
       76  
       60 -**Output and workflow.** QE receives test reports and anomaly 
          -logs. Development teams receive self-check feedback within the
          -ir workflow. The UI/UX team receives threshold-based alerts ro
          -uted through an approval workflow. The content production team
          - receives validation reports on published content. Each output
          - channel is a module on the same backbone.                    
       77 +Playwright drives all browser interaction. When the platform c
          +licks a button, navigates a page, captures a screenshot, or co
          +mpares two visual states, it does so deterministically. There 
          +is no AI interpretation in the action itself. Playwright handl
          +es desktop and mobile viewports, integrates natively with cros
          +s-browser testing infrastructure, and is Microsoft-native, ali
          +gning with the existing Azure environment.                    
       78  
       62 -### Progressive Buildout                                      
       79 +At this layer, the platform also maintains a deterministic map
          + of the application under test: how different parts of the cod
          +ebase relate to each other, which UI components depend on whic
          +h code paths, and which visual tests are relevant to which cha
          +nges. This mapping extends beyond surface-level file associati
          +ons to recursive downstream dependencies, so that a change in 
          +one module triggers tests for everything that module affects, 
          +not just the module itself. This feeds directly into CI/CD int
          +egration: when code changes ship, the platform automatically i
          +dentifies which tests to run rather than executing the entire 
          +suite or relying on manual test selection.                    
       80  
       64 -This architecture does not require a single large-scale deploy
          -ment. The backbone is built once and extended incrementally. E
          -ach consumer group benefits from what was built for the others
          -, and new capabilities compound rather than duplicate. Sephora
          - can start with one consumer group, validate the approach, and
          - expand to the remaining three on the same foundation.        
       81 +All test execution occurs in isolated environments. Docker con
          +tainers provide clean, reproducible execution states for devel
          +opment and testing. In production, this translates to Azure Co
          +ntainer Apps with compute allocated based on actual demand.   
       82  
       66 -### Deterministic Playbook Engine                             
       83 +#### Layer 2: Saved Playbooks and Structured Testing          
       84  
       68 -One of the most valuable capabilities in the platform is the a
          -bility to convert AI-discovered testing workflows into determi
          -nistic, replayable playbooks.                                 
       85 +Once an effective testing workflow has been discovered and val
          +idated, it is saved as a deterministic playbook: an exact, rep
          +eatable sequence of steps, assertions, and validation criteria
          +. Playbooks do not require AI to execute. They are the platfor
          +m's equivalent of recorded automation workflows, except they a
          +re tied to the codebase and mapped to specific features, UI co
          +mponents, and code paths.                                     
       86  
       70 -The process works in stages. First, an AI agent explores a tes
          -ting objective using its tools (browser automation for navigat
          -ion, visual comparison for validation, design file references 
          -for baseline). Once a workflow succeeds and is validated by a 
          -human reviewer, it is saved as a deterministic playbook: an ex
          -act, repeatable sequence of steps, assertions, and validation 
          -criteria.                                                     
       87 +When a release ships or a code change merges, the platform kno
          +ws which playbooks are affected and runs them automatically. I
          +f a feature changes intentionally, the associated playbooks ca
          +n be updated or retired by the team. Tests do not break on dep
          +loyment unless the underlying behavior genuinely changed.     
       88  
       72 -Saved playbooks do not require AI to execute. They run determi
          -nistically, on any schedule, against any environment, with ful
          -l reproducibility. They are tied to specific code changes and 
          -releases so that the system knows which playbooks are relevant
          - when something ships. Tests do not break when features or UI 
          -elements change unless the change genuinely affects the valida
          -ted behavior.                                                 
       89 +Scheduled execution through a workflow orchestration layer (Ap
          +ache Airflow) handles nightly regression suites, post-deploy v
          +alidation sweeps, and periodic production monitoring. CI/CD in
          +tegration through GitHub Actions handles event-driven test tri
          +ggers on code changes, giving development teams a seamless exp
          +erience within their existing workflow.                       
       90  
       74 -This approach combines the strength of AI (exploration, discov
          -ery, adaptability to new situations) with the reliability of d
          -eterministic automation (repeatability, predictability, audita
          -bility). Development teams retain full control: they can revie
          -w, edit, extend, or retire playbooks at any time. The system m
          -akes testing easier without removing human governance.        
       91 +The accumulated playbook library grows over time and represent
          +s a compounding asset: each validated playbook is a testing wo
          +rkflow that no longer requires manual execution.              
       92  
       76 -### Human-in-the-Loop and Confidence Scoring                  
       93 +#### Layer 3: Agentic Exploration and Discovery               
       94  
       78 -Human involvement is a design feature of the platform, not a l
          -imitation to be minimized. The system follows a reinforcement-
          -based confidence model:                                       
       95 +When the platform encounters a new feature, an unfamiliar appl
          +ication state, or a testing objective that no existing playboo
          +k covers, an agentic workflow takes over. The agent uses its t
          +ools (Playwright for browser interaction, visual comparison mo
          +dels for understanding what it sees, design file references fo
          +r baseline comparison) to explore, discover anomalies, and con
          +struct new testing workflows.                                 
       96  
       80 -In the initial phase, human reviewers validate all agent-gener
          -ated results and newly discovered workflows. Each validated su
          -ccess builds the agent's track record. As the track record gro
          -ws, the threshold for human review decreases. Over time, human
          - involvement shifts from reviewing every result to reviewing o
          -nly exceptions and edge cases, similar to the transition from 
          -hands-on testing to managing and overseeing an automated testi
          -ng operation.                                                 
       97 +Exploratory testing is the most visible agentic capability. An
          + agent navigates the application as a user would: visiting pag
          +es, interacting with elements, identifying visual inconsistenc
          +ies, broken layouts, content errors, or accessibility issues. 
          +Every action is logged, every screenshot is captured, and the 
          +results are traceable to the specific environment state and co
          +de version.                                                   
       98  
       82 -A review agent layer sits between the testing agents and human
          - reviewers. This layer filters results before any human sees t
          -hem, ensuring that human attention is spent on genuine issues 
          -rather than noise. The combination of confidence scoring and r
          -eview filtering means the system gets more efficient over time
          - without sacrificing oversight.                               
       99 +Visual understanding models distinguish between intentional de
          +sign changes and genuine defects. A button that moved slightly
          + due to a layout adjustment is not the same as a button that d
          +isappeared. This contextual judgment reduces false positives t
          +hat would otherwise require human triage. At scale, automated 
          +visual consistency exceeds what human reviewers can maintain, 
          +particularly across large volumes of pages, variants, and devi
          +ces where attention fatigue degrades accuracy.                
      100  
       84 -### Control Panel                                             
      101 +When an agent discovers a workflow that successfully validates
          + a testing objective, that workflow becomes a candidate for La
          +yer 2. Once validated by a human reviewer, it is saved as a de
          +terministic playbook. This cycle is what makes the platform se
          +lf-improving: AI explores and discovers, validated discoveries
          + become deterministic assets, the deterministic library grows,
          + and the need for exploration decreases over time for known ar
          +eas while remaining available for new features and changes.   
      102  
       86 -The platform includes a web-based control panel scoped to each
          - user's role and responsibilities. The control panel connects 
          -to the team's code repositories, integrates with existing sing
          -le sign-on infrastructure, and provides visibility into test s
          -tatus, playbook management, and configuration settings.       
      103 +**Guardrails and production safety:** Agents operating in prod
          +uction are restricted to non-destructive actions. They can obs
          +erve, capture, compare, and report, but they cannot modify dat
          +a, submit forms, or alter application state. This restriction 
          +is enforced at the tool access level, not through behavioral i
          +nstructions. In sandbox environments, destructive actions can 
          +be explicitly enabled for testing scenarios that require inter
          +action. The platform adapts its permission model based on the 
          +environment it is operating in.                               
      104  
       88 -The recommended backend framework is Python-based (Django or F
          -astAPI), selected for direct compatibility with the agentic li
          -braries that power the platform (LangGraph, Playwright Python 
          -bindings, Azure SDK). This eliminates translation layers betwe
          -en the web application and the agent infrastructure. If the te
          -am has a strong preference for an alternative framework, BayOn
          -e can adapt accordingly.                                      
      105 +#### Layer 4: Review and Confidence                           
      106  
      107 +Before any agent-generated result reaches a human, it passes t
          +hrough a review layer. A dedicated review agent evaluates the 
          +output for quality, relevance, and actionability. Results that
          + are noise (false positives, known acceptable variations, dupl
          +icate findings) are filtered. Only findings that meet a qualit
          +y threshold are surfaced to human reviewers.                  
      108 +                                                              
      109 +Confidence scoring operates across the entire platform. Every 
          +agent maintains a track record built from human-validated outc
          +omes. When a human approves an agent's finding, that approval 
          +reinforces the pattern and lowers the threshold for future hum
          +an review of similar findings. When a human rejects a finding 
          +or corrects an agent, the correction feeds back to make the ag
          +ent more cautious in that specific area, and the confidence th
          +reshold for that task type increases temporarily until the cor
          +rection is validated through subsequent successful runs. This 
          +bidirectional feedback (approvals lower thresholds, correction
          +s raise them) ensures the system calibrates itself to real-wor
          +ld accuracy standards.                                        
      110 +                                                              
      111 +Agents do not assess themselves or each other. Confidence scor
          +es are derived from human-validated outcomes and defined perfo
          +rmance indicators, not from agent self-evaluation. This is a d
          +eliberate design choice: agent self-assessment tends to oscill
          +ate between extreme optimism and extreme pessimism rather than
          + producing calibrated judgments. Human expertise remains struc
          +turally necessary for course corrections.                     
      112 +                                                              
      113 +#### Layer 5: Observability                                   
      114 +                                                              
      115 +The platform provides full visibility into what is happening a
          +cross all layers. Every action is traceable: which agent perfo
          +rmed it, what tools it used, what environment it operated in, 
          +what code version was being tested, and what the outcome was. 
          +This traceability is the same standard applied to human QE tea
          +ms and provides the audit trail that enterprise quality engine
          +ering requires.                                               
      116 +                                                              
      117 +Beyond individual action tracing, the platform surfaces aggreg
          +ate data: agent performance trends, test pass rates over time,
          + edge case patterns, confidence score trajectories, and playbo
          +ok coverage metrics. When the system is mature, the most inter
          +esting data is the edge cases. Are remaining failures because 
          +the system is too strict? Because the test criteria need updat
          +ing? Because genuine defects are being found? This diagnostic 
          +capability helps the team continuously calibrate the platform 
          +against real-world quality standards.                         
      118 +                                                              
      119 +When tests miss something, those edge cases are captured and f
          +ed back into the system as patterns to watch for in the future
          +, even in situations that are not identical to the original mi
          +ss. This learning loop expands the platform's awareness over t
          +ime, complementing the confidence scoring mechanism that opera
          +tes at the individual agent level.                            
      120 +                                                              
      121 +This observability layer is designed for the organizational tr
          +ansition already underway: QE leadership moving from hands-on 
          +engineering to decision-making and oversight. The platform pro
          +vides the evidence base needed to manage automated testing ope
          +rations with confidence.                                      
      122 +                                                              
      123 +### Configuration Across Consumer Groups                      
      124 +                                                              
      125 +The layered architecture is the same for all consumer groups. 
          +What differs is configuration across four dimensions: baseline
          + reference, tolerance and precision, workflow integration, and
          + tool connectors.                                             
      126 +                                                              
      127 +Tool connectors extend the platform's reach without modifying 
          +the core. Connectors for Figma, Canva, WordPress, and other to
          +ols are available via Model Context Protocol (MCP), and additi
          +onal connectors can be built as needed. Each new connector exp
          +ands what the platform can do for all teams.                  
      128 +                                                              
      129 +### Unified Platform Hub                                      
      130 +                                                              
      131 +The platform provides a centralized interface where all teams 
          +and leadership have unified visibility into test status, playb
          +ook management, configuration settings, and performance metric
          +s without navigating multiple applications. This hub consolida
          +tes what would otherwise require separate custom tools for eac
          +h consumer group, reducing technical debt and ensuring that ev
          +ery team operates from the same data. The hub integrates with 
          +existing code repositories and single sign-on infrastructure, 
          +scoped to each user's role and responsibilities.              
      132 +                                                              
      133 +**System autonomy, not autonomous systems:** The platform is a
          + system of coordinated components (deterministic processes, ag
          +entic capabilities, human oversight, and observability) that c
          +ollectively achieves autonomous behavior while remaining fully
          + traceable and governable. No single agent operates independen
          +tly or without oversight. The autonomy is a property of the sy
          +stem's design, not of any individual component within it.     
      134 +                                                              
      135 +### Deterministic Playbook Engine                             
      136 +                                                              
      137 +1. AI Exploration: An agent explores a testing objective using
          + browser automation, visual comparison, and design file refere
          +nces.                                                         
      138 +2. Validation and Save: Once a workflow succeeds and is valida
          +ted by a human reviewer, it is saved as a deterministic playbo
          +ok.                                                           
      139 +3. Deterministic Replay: Saved playbooks run without AI, on an
          +y schedule, against any environment, mapped to code changes an
          +d releases.                                                   
      140 +                                                              
      141 +Playbooks are assets owned by the teams. Teams can review, edi
          +t, extend, or retire playbooks. The platform makes testing mor
          +e efficient without removing human governance.                
      142 +                                                              
      143 +### Human-in-the-Loop as a Design Feature                     
      144 +                                                              
      145 +Human involvement is a design feature that makes the system tr
          +ustworthy, not a limitation to be minimized. In the initial ph
          +ase, human reviewers validate all agent-generated results and 
          +newly discovered workflows. Each validated success builds the 
          +agent's track record. As the record grows, the threshold for m
          +andatory human review decreases for specific agent and task co
          +mbinations that have proven reliable.                         
      146 +                                                              
      147 +The result is a system where human effort decreases naturally 
          +as trust is earned. The human role transitions from reviewing 
          +every result to overseeing the system's overall health, interv
          +ening on exceptions, and making course corrections when agents
          + drift.

‚óŹ All 8 files read. Key takeaways for the improved diagram:

  1. classDef with :::className is much cleaner than individual style statements
   (from 06)
  2. FontAwesome icons (fa:fa-brain, fa:fa-gears, etc.) with htmlLabels: true
  give visual context (from 04, 05)
  3. Bold + bullet pattern (<b>Title</b><br/>‚Ėł Detail) creates information-dense
   but readable nodes (from 04)
  4. Direction overrides inside subgraphs (direction LR) can make layers
  horizontal while the overall flow is TB (from 07)
  5. Invisible links (~~~) control spacing without visual connections (from 03)
  6. Simpler is better - the combined example in 05 uses clean category-based
  subgraphs without cramming too many details

  For the architecture diagram, I should:
  - Use classDef instead of individual style lines
  - Add FontAwesome icons to key nodes
  - Use direction LR inside each layer subgraph so nodes within a layer sit
  side-by-side (compact rows) while layers stack vertically
  - Simplify node labels (bold title + one line, not three-line descriptions)
  - Use invisible links to control width

  and then create the concise version. Starting with the diagram fix.

‚óŹ Good call to brainstorm on this. There are a few different approaches and I
  want to think through the trade-offs with you.

  The core problem: The architecture detail document has 5 diagrams, some with
  wide aspect ratios (especially the LR-direction ones like the discovery cycle
  and confidence scoring). In portrait mode on 8.5x11, these get compressed to
  fit the page width, making them tiny and illegible. The edge labels
  ("Approve", "Correct", "Discovery", etc.) get truncated because mermaid
  calculates their box size before rendering, and when the SVG is scaled down to
   fit a narrow container, the labels clip.

  Option A: Landscape document (11x8.5)
  Change the @page CSS to size: 11in 8.5in and reflow the whole document for
  landscape. Gives diagrams more horizontal room. Downside: the text-heavy
  sections (layer descriptions, playbook engine prose) would have very wide
  lines that are harder to read in landscape. Mixing text and diagrams in
  landscape is generally awkward.

  Option B: Mixed orientation with CSS page selectors
  Keep the document portrait for text sections. For diagram pages, wrap them in
  a container with a named page rule that switches to landscape just for that
  page when printing. CSS supports this:
  @page landscape { size: 11in 8.5in; }
  .landscape-page { page: landscape; page-break-before: always;
  page-break-after: always; }
  This way, text pages print portrait, diagram pages print landscape. Each
  diagram gets a full landscape page to itself. Downside: browser support for
  named @page rules is inconsistent. Chrome handles it well, Firefox and Safari
  are hit-or-miss.

  Option C: Rotate just the diagram SVG 90 degrees
  Keep the document portrait. For wide diagrams, apply a CSS transform
  (transform: rotate(-90deg)) to the SVG container so the diagram renders
  sideways on a portrait page. The diagram itself would be landscape-oriented
  within a portrait sheet, similar to how you would rotate a chart in a Word
  document. Downside: this is a visual hack. It looks fine on screen and prints
  correctly, but the surrounding text needs to be above/below the rotated
  diagram, not beside it. And the sizing math gets tricky.

  Option D: Separate the diagrams into their own file
  Pull all diagrams out of the architecture detail document into a standalone
  architecture_diagrams_2026-04-14.html that is a landscape-only document (full
  11x8.5). Each diagram gets a full page with a title and brief description. The
   architecture detail prose document stays portrait with references like "See
  Diagram 1 in the Architecture Diagrams companion." Downside: two files where
  there was one. But the diagrams would be fully legible, printable, and
  shareable independently.

  On the truncated edge labels: That is a separate issue from page orientation.
  Mermaid calculates edge label box dimensions during render, and if the SVG is
  then scaled by CSS to fit a smaller container, the labels clip. Fixes include:
   increasing rankSpacing further (we are at 70, could try 90), shortening the
  label text (e.g., "Approved" instead of "Approve"), or using the post-render
  JavaScript to find edge label elements and increase their width. The most
  reliable fix is shorter labels combined with higher rankSpacing.

  My instinct is that Option B (mixed orientation) would be ideal if it works in
   the target browser, or Option D (separate diagram file) if we want guaranteed
   results across all browsers and printing. What is your thinking?

‚úĽ Cogitated for 31s

‚ĚĮ I'm not saying to do it in a hacky way and do it with an SVG transform. I'm   
  asking you if mermaid charts can be done in a rotated fashion, or if they

  Do not shorten the label text because of truncated edge labels. We need an

  it, regardless of how long or short it is.                                    

‚óŹ Mermaid does not have a native rotation parameter. The only layout control is
  the graph direction: graph TB (top-bottom), graph LR (left-right), graph BT
  (bottom-top), graph RL (right-left). There is no rotate: 90deg or equivalent
  config option. So "rotating a diagram with mermaid" means changing an LR
  diagram to TB (or vice versa), which changes the conceptual flow direction,
  not just the visual orientation.

  For the edge label truncation, that is a mermaid rendering bug where the label
   rect is sized before the text fully measures. The fix is post-render
  JavaScript that finds every .edgeLabel element, measures the actual text, and
  resizes the rect to fit. No shortening, no workarounds.

  Let me make a prototype that tests both: a landscape page format (11x8.5, all
  pages) with the edge label fix applied via post-render JS.

‚óŹ The difference between A and B:

  Option A: Organized by team. Four nodes stacked vertically. Each node IS a
  consumer group (QE in blue, Dev in green, UI/UX in purple, Producers in
  orange). Inside each node, the four dimensions are listed as bullets. So the
  QE node would say: "baseline: prior app state, tolerance: broad, workflow:
  test reports to pipeline, connectors: BrowserStack." You read it as "here is
  everything about how QE uses the platform."

  Option B: Organized by dimension. Four nodes stacked vertically. Each node IS
  a dimension (Baseline Reference, Tolerance, Workflow Integration, Tool
  Connectors). Inside each node, the four consumer groups are listed as bullets
  showing how each team uses that dimension. So the Baseline Reference node
  would say: "QE: prior app state, Dev: Figma designs, UI/UX: component
  standards, Producers: expected content." You read it as "here is how baseline
  reference works differently for each team."

  The difference is what you are grouping by: A groups by team, B groups by
  dimension. Same information, different reading experience. A answers "what
  does each team get?" B answers "how does each setting vary across teams?"

  On the page sizing: I should not have picked 6in and 7in. For 8.5x11 with
  half-inch margins, the usable area is 7.5in wide by 10in tall. The diagram
  title and caption need maybe 0.5in, leaving 9.5in of vertical space for the
  SVG. Do you want each diagram to fill that full 9.5in height, or should I

‚óŹ The problem is that mermaid sets explicit width and height pixel attributes on
   the SVG element during rendering (like width="450" height="600"). Those pixel
   values override any CSS sizing. No amount of CSS will make the SVG scale
  because the hard-coded attributes take priority.

  The fix is post-render JavaScript that:
  1. Reads the SVG's natural pixel dimensions from those attributes
  2. Converts them into a viewBox (which enables scaling)
  3. Removes the fixed pixel attributes
  4. Lets CSS width: 100%; height: 100% actually work

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Update(sephora/qa_qe_playwright/deliverables/architecture_detail_v2_2026-04-14
        .html)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Added 17 lines
      517              }
      518            }
      519          });
      520 +                                                              
      521 +        // Scale SVG to fill container: convert fixed pixel di
          +mensions to viewBox                                           
      522 +        const w = svg.getAttribute('width');                  
      523 +        const h = svg.getAttribute('height');                 
      524 +        if (w && h) {                                         
      525 +          const numW = parseFloat(w);                         
      526 +          const numH = parseFloat(h);                         
      527 +          if (!svg.getAttribute('viewBox')) {                 
      528 +            svg.setAttribute('viewBox', '0 0 ' + numW + ' ' + 
          +numH);                                                        
      529 +          }                                                   
      530 +          svg.removeAttribute('width');                       
      531 +          svg.removeAttribute('height');                      
      532 +          svg.style.width = '100%';                           
      533 +          svg.style.height = '100%';                          
      534 +          svg.style.maxWidth = '100%';                        
      535 +          svg.style.maxHeight = '100%';                       
      536 +        }                                                     
      537        });
      538      }

  - Figure 1 (Platform Architecture): Complete, all 7 nodes visible with colors
  and title bars. Chart doesn't fill the frame but is readable.

  9-inch viewport height. Those two need either fewer bullet items per node to

‚óŹ Agent "Research SVG scaling in browsers" completed

‚óŹ SVG scaling research is back. Two more still running. I'll wait for all three
  before presenting findings.

‚óŹ Agent "Research mermaid SVG scaling" completed

‚óŹ Two of three are back. The mermaid research has the answer: mermaid sets an
  inline max-width style that caps the SVG at its natural rendered width. The
  SVG will shrink in a narrow container but never grow beyond its computed size.
   The fix is max-width: none !important on the SVG to remove that cap. Waiting
  for the Playwright research to complete, then I'll present all findings
  together.

‚óŹ Agent "Research Playwright screenshot scaling" completed

‚óŹ All three are back. Here is what I learned:

  The root cause: Mermaid sets an inline style="max-width: {N}px" on the SVG it
  generates, where N is the natural rendered width of the chart content. The SVG
   has width="100%" and a viewBox, so it is technically scalable, but the inline
   max-width caps it at its natural size. The SVG will shrink in a narrow
  container but will never grow beyond that cap. That is why the charts render
  small in a large viewport.

  The fix: Remove the max-width cap. One line of CSS: max-width: none !important
   on the mermaid SVG. With the cap removed, width="100%" takes effect and the
  SVG scales to fill its container. The viewBox handles the coordinate mapping
  so everything scales proportionally.

  For Playwright capture: The element-level screenshot via locator.screenshot()
  captures just the SVG element at its rendered size, respecting
  device_scale_factor. So the workflow is: render the chart in an HTML file
  where the SVG fills the page, use locator.screenshot() to capture just the SVG
   element at 2x scale.

  All three research documents are in the planning folder:
  - mermaid_svg_scaling_research_2026-04-14.md
  - svg_scaling_browser_research_2026-04-14.md
  - playwright_screenshot_research_2026-04-14.md

  The fix to the chart HTML files and the screenshot script is straightforward.
  Do you want me to apply it to one chart file first, run the screenshot, and
  show you the result?
